href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>.</div>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">An unknown physical entity (lato sensu) that causes the expansion of the universe to accelerate. It might be a completely new component of physics or a broader extension of known physics, for example, gravity. </div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" Zeldovich">Zeldovich</a></li>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#ddd8e7;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology#Subject_history" title="Physical cosmology">Subject history</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Discovery of cosmic microwave background radiation">Discovery of cosmic microwave<br/>background radiation</a></div></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="History of the Big Bang theory">History of the Big Bang theory</a></li>\n<li><div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Religious_interpretations_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="Religious interpretations of the Big Bang theory">Religious interpretations of<br/>the Big Bang theory</a></div></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_cosmological_theories" title="Timeline of cosmological theories">Timeline of cosmological theories</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;display:block;margin-top:0.4em; line-height:1.6em;padding-bottom:0.5em;">\n<ul><li><img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Category:Physical_cosmology" title="Category:Physical cosmology">Category</a></li>\n<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png"><img alt="Ilc 9yr moll4096.png" class="noviewer" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/16px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/24px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/32px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Cosmology" title="Portal:Cosmology">Cosmology portal</a></li>\n<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crab_Nebula.jpg"><img alt="Crab Nebula.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="3864" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/16px-Crab_Nebula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/24px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/32px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Astronomy" title="Portal:Astronomy">Astronomy portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Physical_cosmology" title="Template talk:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">physical cosmology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <b>dark energy</b> is an unknown form of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> which is <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesized</a> to permeate all of space, tending to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">accelerate</a> the <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">expansion of the universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170220_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170220-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-2">[2]</a></sup>  Dark energy is the most accepted hypothesis to explain the observations since the 1990s indicating that the universe is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expanding</a> at an <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">accelerating rate</a>.\n</p><p>Assuming that the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">standard model of cosmology</a> is correct, the <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)#2013_data_release" title="Planck (spacecraft)">best current measurements</a> indicate that dark energy contributes 68% of the total energy in the present-day <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>. The mass&#8211;energy of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baryon#Baryonic_matter" title="Baryon">ordinary (baryonic)</a> <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> contribute 27% and 5%, respectively, and other components such as <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> contribute a very small amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_overview_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck_overview-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_overview2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck_overview2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7parameters_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7parameters-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkMatter_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-DarkMatter-6">[6]</a></sup> The density of dark energy is very low (~ 7 &#215; 10<sup>&#8722;30</sup> g/cm<sup>3</sup>)  much less than the density of ordinary matter or dark matter within galaxies. However, it dominates the mass&#8211;energy of the universe because it is uniform across space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two proposed forms for dark energy are the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carroll_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-carroll-11">[11]</a></sup> representing a constant energy density filling space homogeneously, and <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field_theory" title="Scalar field theory">scalar fields</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Quintessence_(physics)" title="Quintessence (physics)">quintessence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moduli_(physics)" title="Moduli (physics)">moduli</a>, dynamic quantities whose energy density can vary in time and space. Contributions from scalar fields that are constant in space are usually also included in the cosmological constant. The cosmological constant can be formulated to be equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Zero-point_energy" title="Zero-point energy">zero-point radiation</a> of space i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Scalar fields that change in space can be difficult to distinguish from a cosmological constant because the change may be extremely slow.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History_of_discovery_and_previous_speculation"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History of discovery and previous speculation</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Einstein\'s_cosmological_constant"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Einstein\'s cosmological class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Nature"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Nature</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a Sachs-Wolfe effect</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Observational_Hubble_constant_data"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Observational Hubble constant data</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Theories_of_dark_energy"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Theories of dark energy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Cosmological_constant"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Cosmological constant</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Quintessence"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Quintessence</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Interacting_dark_energy"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Interacting dark energy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Variable_dark_energy_models"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Variable dark energy models</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Observational_skepticism"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Observational skepticism</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Other_mechanism_driving_acceleration"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Other mechanism driving acceleration</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Modified_gravity"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Modified gravity</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Implications_for_the_fate_of_the_universe"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Implications for the fate of the universe</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#In_philosophy_of_science"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">In philosophy of science</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_discovery_and_previous_speculation">History of discovery and previous speculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of discovery and previous speculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Einstein.27s_cosmological_constant"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Einstein\'s_cosmological_constant">Einstein\'s cosmological constant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Einstein\'s cosmological constant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The "cosmological constant" is a constant term that can be added to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein%27s_field_equation" title="Einstein\'s field equation">Einstein\'s field equation</a> of general relativity.  If considered as a "source term" in the field equation, it can be viewed as equivalent to the mass of empty space (which conceptually could be either positive or negative), or "<a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a>".\n</p><p>The cosmological constant was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a> as a mechanism to obtain a solution of the gravitational <a href="/wiki/Field_equation" title="Field equation">field equation</a> that would lead to a static universe, effectively using dark energy to balance gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein-13">[13]</a></sup> Einstein gave the cosmological constant the symbol &#923; (capital lambda). Einstein stated that the cosmological constant required that `empty space takes the role of gravitating <a href="/wiki/Negative_mass" title="Negative mass">negative masses</a> which are distributed all over the interstellar space\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>.\n</p><p>The mechanism was an example of <a href="/wiki/Fine-tuning" title="Fine-tuning">fine-tuning</a>, and it was later realized that Einstein\'s static universe would not be stable: local inhomogeneities would ultimately lead to either the runaway expansion or contraction of the universe. The <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_equilibrium" title="Dynamic equilibrium">equilibrium</a> is unstable: if the universe expands slightly, then the expansion releases vacuum energy, which causes yet more expansion. Likewise, a universe which contracts slightly will continue contracting. These sorts of disturbances are inevitable, due to the uneven distribution of matter throughout the universe. Further, observations made by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> in 1929 showed that the universe appears to be expanding and not static at all. Einstein reportedly referred to his failure to predict the idea of a dynamic universe, in contrast to a static universe, as his greatest blunder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflationary_dark_energy">Inflationary dark energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Inflationary dark energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Alan_Guth" title="Alan Guth">Alan Guth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexei_Starobinsky" title="Alexei Starobinsky">Alexei Starobinsky</a> proposed in 1980 that a negative pressure field, similar in concept to dark energy, could drive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">cosmic inflation</a> in the very early universe. Inflation postulates that some repulsive force, qualitatively similar to dark energy, resulted in an enormous and exponential expansion of the universe slightly after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. Such expansion is an essential feature of most current models of the Big Bang. However, inflation must have occurred at a much higher energy density than the dark energy we observe today and is thought to have completely ended when the universe was just a fraction of a second old. It is unclear what relation, if any, exists between dark energy and inflation. Even after inflationary models became accepted, the cosmological constant was thought to be irrelevant to the current universe.\n</p><p>Nearly all inflation models predict that the total (matter+energy) density of the universe should be very close to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_density_(cosmology)" title="Critical density (cosmology)">critical density</a>. During the 1980s, most cosmological research focused on models with critical density in matter only, usually 95% <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> and 5% ordinary matter (baryons). These models were found to be successful at forming realistic galaxies and clusters, but some problems appeared in the late 1980s: in particular, the model required a value for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_constant" title="Hubble constant">Hubble constant</a> lower than preferred by observations, and the model under-predicted observations of large-scale galaxy clustering. These difficulties became stronger after the discovery of anisotropy in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer" title="Cosmic Background Explorer">COBE</a> spacecraft in 1992, and several modified CDM models came under active study through the mid-1990s: these included the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a> and a mixed cold/hot dark matter model.  The first direct evidence for dark energy came from supernova observations in 1998 of <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">accelerated expansion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Adam_Riess" title="Adam Riess">Riess</a> <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riess_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-riess-17">[17]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Saul_Perlmutter" title="Saul Perlmutter">Perlmutter</a> <i>et al.</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perlmutter_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-perlmutter-18">[18]</a></sup> and the Lambda-CDM model then became the leading model. Soon after, dark energy was supported by independent observations: in 2000, the <a href="/wiki/BOOMERanG_experiment" title="BOOMERanG experiment">BOOMERanG</a> and <a href="/wiki/Millimeter_Anisotropy_eXperiment_IMaging_Array" title="Millimeter Anisotropy eXperiment IMaging Array">Maxima</a> cosmic microwave background experiments observed the first <a href="/wiki/Baryon_acoustic_oscillations" title="Baryon acoustic oscillations">acoustic peak</a> in the CMB, showing that the total (matter+energy) density is close to 100% of critical density. Then in 2001, the <a href="/wiki/2dF_Galaxy_Redshift_Survey" title="2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey">2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey</a> gave strong evidence that the matter density is around 30% of critical. The large difference between these two supports a smooth component of dark energy making up the difference. Much more precise measurements from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> in 2003&#8211;2010 have continued to support the standard model and give more accurate measurements of the key parameters.\n</p><p>The term "dark energy", echoing <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a>\'s "dark matter" from the 1930s, was coined by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Turner_(cosmologist)" title="Michael Turner (cosmologist)">Michael Turner</a> in 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Change_in_expansion_over_time">Change in expansion over time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Change in expansion over time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dark_Energy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="608" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Dark_Energy.jpg/220px-Dark_Energy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Dark_Energy.jpg/330px-Dark_Energy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Dark_Energy.jpg/440px-Dark_Energy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dark_Energy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram representing the accelerated expansion of the universe due to dark energy.</div></div></div>\n<p>High-precision measurements of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expansion of the universe</a> are required to understand how the expansion rate changes over time and space. In <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, the evolution of the expansion rate is estimated from the <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">curvature of the universe</a> and the cosmological <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state_(cosmology)" title="Equation of state (cosmology)">equation of state</a> (the relationship between temperature, pressure, and combined matter, energy, and vacuum energy density for any region of space). Measuring the equation of state for dark energy is one of the biggest efforts in observational cosmology today. Adding the cosmological constant to cosmology\'s standard <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">FLRW metric</a> leads to the Lambda-CDM model, which has been referred to as the "<i>standard model of cosmology</i>" because of its precise agreement with observations.\n</p><p>As of 2013, the Lambda-CDM model is consistent with a series of increasingly rigorous cosmological observations, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_spacecraft" title="Planck spacecraft">Planck spacecraft</a> and the Supernova Legacy Survey. First results from the SNLS reveal that the average behavior (i.e., equation of state) of dark energy behaves like Einstein\'s cosmological constant to a precision of 10%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snls_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-snls-20">[20]</a></sup> Recent results from the Hubble Space Telescope Higher-Z Team indicate that dark energy has been present for at least 9 billion years and during the period preceding cosmic acceleration.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature">Nature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The nature of dark energy is more hypothetical than that of dark matter, and many things about it remain matters of speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Dark energy is thought to be very homogeneous and not very <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">dense</a>, and is not known to interact through any of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_forces" title="Fundamental forces">fundamental forces</a> other than <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Since it is quite rarefied and un-massive &#8212; roughly 10<sup>&#8722;27</sup> kg/m<sup>3</sup> &#8212; it is unlikely to be detectable in laboratory experiments. The reason dark energy can have such a profound effect on the universe, making up 68% of universal density in spite of being so dilute, is that it uniformly fills otherwise empty space.\n</p><p>Independently of its actual nature, dark energy would need to have a strong negative pressure (repulsive action), like <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Metamaterial" title="Metamaterial">metamaterial</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> to explain the observed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">acceleration</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expansion of the universe</a>. According to general relativity, the pressure within a substance contributes to its gravitational attraction for other objects just as its mass density does. This happens because the physical quantity that causes matter to generate gravitational effects is the <a href="/wiki/Stress%E2%80%93energy_tensor" title="Stress&#8211;energy tensor">stress&#8211;energy tensor</a>, which contains both the energy (or matter) density of a substance and its pressure and viscosity<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (February 2017)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Dark_energy#Dubious" title="Talk:Dark energy">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>. In the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric</a>, it can be shown that a strong constant negative pressure in all the universe causes an acceleration in the expansion if the universe is already expanding, or a deceleration in contraction if the universe is already contracting. This accelerating expansion effect is sometimes labeled "gravitational repulsion".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_definition">Technical definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Technical definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations" title="Friedmann equations">Friedmann equations</a></div>\n<p>In standard cosmology, there are three components of the universe: matter, radiation, and dark energy. Matter is anything whose energy density scales with the inverse cube of the scale factor, i.e., &#961; &#8733; a<sup>&#8722;3</sup>, while radiation is anything which scales to the inverse fourth power of the scale factor (&#961; &#8733; a<sup>&#8722;4</sup>). This can be understood intuitively: for an ordinary particle in a square box, doubling the length of a side of the box decreases the density (and hence energy density) by a factor of eight (2<sup>3</sup>). For radiation, the decrease in energy density is greater, because an increase in spatial distance also causes a redshift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The final component, dark energy, is an intrinsic property of space, and so has a constant energy density regardless of the volume under consideration (&#961; &#8733; a<sup>0</sup>). Thus, unlike ordinary matter, it does not get diluted with the expansion of space.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_of_existence">Evidence of existence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Evidence of existence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The evidence for dark energy is indirect but comes from three independent sources:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Distance measurements and their relation to redshift, which suggest the universe has expanded more in the last half of its life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durrer_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durrer-24">[24]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The theoretical need for a type of additional energy that is not matter or dark matter to form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observationally_flat_universe" title="Observationally flat universe">observationally flat universe</a> (absence of any detectable global curvature).</li>\n<li>Measures of large-scale wave-patterns of mass density in the universe.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supernovae">Supernovae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Supernovae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SN1994D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2608" data-file-width="2608" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/220px-SN1994D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/330px-SN1994D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/440px-SN1994D.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SN1994D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Type Ia supernova (bright spot on the bottom-left) near a galaxy</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1998, the <a href="/wiki/High-Z_Supernova_Search_Team" title="High-Z Supernova Search Team">High-Z Supernova Search Team</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riess_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-riess-17">[17]</a></sup> published observations of <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia</a> ("one-A") <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>. In 1999, the <a href="/wiki/Supernova_Cosmology_Project" title="Supernova Cosmology Project">Supernova Cosmology Project</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perlmutter_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-perlmutter-18">[18]</a></sup> followed by suggesting that the expansion of the universe is <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">accelerating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paalhorvathlukacs_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-paalhorvathlukacs-25">[25]</a></sup> The 2011 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Physics" title="List of Nobel laureates in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Saul_Perlmutter" title="Saul Perlmutter">Saul Perlmutter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brian_P._Schmidt" title="Brian P. Schmidt">Brian P. Schmidt</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adam_G._Riess" title="Adam G. Riess">Adam G. Riess</a> for their leadership in the discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N11_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-N11-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since then, these observations have been corroborated by several independent sources. Measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lensing</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">large-scale structure of the cosmos</a>, as well as improved measurements of supernovae, have been consistent with the Lambda-CDM model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmap-28">[28]</a></sup> Some people argue that the only indications for the existence of dark energy are observations of distance measurements and their associated redshifts. Cosmic microwave background anisotropies and baryon acoustic oscillations serve only to demonstrate that distances to a given redshift are larger than would be expected from a "dusty" Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre universe and the local measured Hubble constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-durrer_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-durrer-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Supernovae are useful for cosmology because they are excellent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_candle" title="Standard candle">standard candles</a> across cosmological distances. They allow researchers to measure the expansion history of the universe by looking at the relationship between the distance to an object and its <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>, which gives how fast it is receding from us. The relationship is roughly linear, according to <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a>. It is relatively easy to measure redshift, but finding the distance to an object is more difficult. Usually, astronomers use standard candles: objects for which the intrinsic brightness, or <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a>, is known. This allows the object\'s distance to be measured from its actual observed brightness, or <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a>. Type Ia supernovae are the best-known standard candles across cosmological distances because of their extreme and consistent <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a>.\n</p><p>Recent observations of supernovae are consistent with a universe made up 71.3% of dark energy and 27.4% of a combination of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kowalski2008_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kowalski2008-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_microwave_background">Cosmic microwave background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cosmic microwave background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WMAP_2008_universe_content.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3900" data-file-width="2763" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/WMAP_2008_universe_content.png/170px-WMAP_2008_universe_content.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/WMAP_2008_universe_content.png/255px-WMAP_2008_universe_content.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/WMAP_2008_universe_content.png/340px-WMAP_2008_universe_content.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WMAP_2008_universe_content.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimated division of total energy in the universe into matter, dark matter and dark energy based on five years of WMAP data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The existence of dark energy, in whatever form, is needed to reconcile the measured geometry of space with the total amount of matter in the universe. Measurements of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> (CMB) <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropies</a> indicate that the universe is close to <a href="/wiki/Flatness_problem" title="Flatness problem">flat</a>. For the <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">shape of the universe</a> to be flat, the mass-energy density of the universe must be equal to the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations#Density_parameter" title="Friedmann equations">critical density</a>. The total amount of matter in the universe (including <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>), as measured from the CMB spectrum, accounts for only about 30% of the critical density. This implies the existence of an additional form of energy to account for the remaining 70%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-wmap-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> (WMAP) spacecraft <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe#Seven-year_data_release" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">seven-year analysis</a> estimated a universe made up of 72.8% dark energy, 22.7% dark matter, and 4.5% ordinary matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7parameters_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7parameters-5">[5]</a></sup>\nWork done in 2013 based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_spacecraft" title="Planck spacecraft">Planck spacecraft</a> observations of the CMB gave a more accurate estimate of 68.3% dark energy, 26.8% dark matter, and 4.9% ordinary matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washington_Post_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_Post-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Large-scale_structure">Large-scale structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Large-scale structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The theory of <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#Large-scale_structure" title="Observable universe">large-scale structure</a>, which governs the formation of structures in the universe (<a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">galaxy groups and clusters</a>), also suggests that the density of matter in the universe is only 30% of the critical density.\n</p><p>A 2011 survey, the WiggleZ galaxy survey of more than 200,000 galaxies, provided further evidence towards the existence of dark energy, although the exact physics behind it remains unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-real_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-real-34">[34]</a></sup> The WiggleZ survey from the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Astronomical_Observatory" title="Australian Astronomical Observatory">Australian Astronomical Observatory</a> scanned the galaxies to determine their redshift. Then, by exploiting the fact that <a href="/wiki/Baryon_acoustic_oscillations" title="Baryon acoustic oscillations">baryon acoustic oscillations</a> have left <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a> regularly of ~150 Mpc diameter, surrounded by the galaxies, the voids were used as standard rulers to estimate distances to galaxies as far as 2,000 Mpc (redshift 0.6), allowing for accurate estimate of the speeds of galaxies from their redshift and distance. The data confirmed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_acceleration" title="Cosmic acceleration">cosmic acceleration</a> up to half of the age of the universe (7 billion years) and constrain its inhomogeneity to 1 part in 10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-real_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-real-34">[34]</a></sup> This provides a confirmation to cosmic acceleration independent of supernovae.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late-time_integrated_Sachs-Wolfe_effect">Late-time integrated Sachs-Wolfe effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Late-time integrated Sachs-Wolfe effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Accelerated cosmic expansion causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_well" title="Gravitational potential well">gravitational potential wells</a> and hills to flatten as <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> pass through them, producing cold spots and hot spots on the CMB aligned with vast supervoids and superclusters. This so-called late-time <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrated_Sachs%E2%80%93Wolfe_effect" title="Integrated Sachs&#8211;Wolfe effect">Integrated Sachs&#8211;Wolfe effect (ISW)</a> is a direct signal of dark energy in a flat universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> It was reported at high significance in 2008 by Ho <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and Giannantonio <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_Hubble_constant_data">Observational Hubble constant data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Observational Hubble constant data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A new approach to test evidence of dark energy through observational <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_constant" title="Hubble constant">Hubble constant</a> data (OHD) has gained significant attention in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The Hubble constant, <i>H</i>(<i>z</i>), is measured as a function of cosmological <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>. OHD directly tracks the expansion history of the universe by taking passively evolving early-type galaxies as &#8220;cosmic chronometers&#8221;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> From this point, this approach provides standard clocks in the universe. The core of this idea is the measurement of the differential age evolution as a function of redshift of these cosmic chronometers. Thus, it provides a direct estimate of the Hubble parameter\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H(z)=-{\\frac {1}{1+z}}{\\frac {dz}{dt}}\\approx -{\\frac {1}{1+z}}{\\frac {\\Delta z}{\\Delta t}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>z</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>z</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H(z)=-{\\frac {1}{1+z}}{\\frac {dz}{dt}}\\approx -{\\frac {1}{1+z}}{\\frac {\\Delta z}{\\Delta t}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle H(z)=-{\\frac {1}{1+z}}{\\frac {dz}{dt}}\\approx -{\\frac {1}{1+z}}{\\frac {\\Delta z}{\\Delta t}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/15e57c653b7b16dc8b0a052ca9dcae5f6bab4470" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:34.276ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The reliance on a differential quantity, <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">&#916;<i>z</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">&#916;<i>t</i></span></span>, can minimize many common issues and systematic effects; and as a direct measurement of the Hubble parameter instead of its integral, like <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baryon_acoustic_oscillations" title="Baryon acoustic oscillations">baryon acoustic oscillations</a> (BAO), it brings more information and is appealing in computation. For these reasons, it has been widely used to examine the accelerated cosmic expansion and study properties of dark energy.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_dark_energy">Theories of dark energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Theories of dark energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Dark energy\'s status as a hypothetical force with unknown properties makes it a very active target of research. The problem is attacked from a great variety of angles, such as modifying the prevailing theory of gravity (general relativity), attempting to pin down the properties of dark energy, and finding alternative ways to explain the observational data.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wz-z.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="809" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Wz-z.jpg/220px-Wz-z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Wz-z.jpg/330px-Wz-z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Wz-z.jpg/440px-Wz-z.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wz-z.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The equation of state of Dark Energy for 4 common models by Redshift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <br/> A: CPL Model, <br/> B: Jassal Model, <br/> C: Barboza &amp; Alcaniz Model,<br/> D: Wetterich Model</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmological_constant">Cosmological constant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cosmological constant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">Cosmological constant</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state_(cosmology)" title="Equation of state (cosmology)">Equation of state (cosmology)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DMPie_2013.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="341" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/DMPie_2013.svg/220px-DMPie_2013.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/DMPie_2013.svg/330px-DMPie_2013.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/DMPie_2013.svg/440px-DMPie_2013.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DMPie_2013.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimated distribution of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> in the universe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa-44">[44]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The simplest explanation for dark energy is that it is an intrinsic, fundamental energy of space. This is the cosmological constant, usually represented by the Greek letter &#923; (Lambda, hence <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>). Since energy and mass are related according to the equation <i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup>, Einstein\'s theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> predicts that this energy will have a gravitational effect. It is sometimes called a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a> because it is the energy density of empty <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>.\n</p><p>The cosmological constant has negative pressure equal to its <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> and so causes the expansion of the universe to <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">accelerate</a>. The reason a cosmological constant has negative pressure can be seen from classical thermodynamics. In general, energy must be lost from inside a container (the container must do work on its environment) in order for the volume to increase. Specifically, a change in volume <i>dV</i> requires work done equal to a change of energy &#8722;<i>P&#160;dV</i>, where <i>P</i> is the pressure. But the amount of energy in a container full of vacuum actually increases when the volume increases, because the energy is equal to <i>&#961;V</i>, where <i>&#961;</i> is the energy density of the cosmological constant. Therefore, <i>P</i> is negative and, in fact, <i>P</i>&#160;=&#160;&#8722;<i>&#961;</i>.\n</p><p>There are two major advantages for the cosmological constant. The first is that it is simple. Einstein had in fact introduced this term in his original formulation of general relativity such as to get a static universe. Although he later discarded the term after <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Hubble</a> found that the universe is expanding, a nonzero cosmological constant can act as dark energy, without otherwise changing the Einstein field equations. The other advantage is that there is a natural explanation for its origin. Most <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theories</a> predict <a href="/wiki/Quantum_fluctuation" title="Quantum fluctuation">vacuum fluctuations</a> that would give the vacuum this sort of energy. This is related to the <a href="/wiki/Casimir_effect" title="Casimir effect">Casimir effect</a>, in which there is a small suction into regions where virtual particles are geometrically inhibited from forming (e.g. between plates with tiny separation).\n</p><p>A major outstanding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="Unsolved problems in physics">problem</a> is that the same <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theories</a> predict a huge <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>, more than 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude" title="Orders of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> too large.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carroll_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-carroll-11">[11]</a></sup> This would need to be almost, but not exactly, cancelled by an equally large term of the opposite sign. Some <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetric</a> theories require a cosmological constant that is exactly zero,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> which does not help because supersymmetry must be broken.\n</p><p>Nonetheless, the cosmological constant is the most <a href="/wiki/Occam%27s_razor" title="Occam\'s razor">economical solution</a> to the problem of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_acceleration" title="Cosmic acceleration">cosmic acceleration</a>. Thus, the current standard model of cosmology, the Lambda-CDM model, includes the cosmological constant as an essential feature.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quintessence">Quintessence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Quintessence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quintessence_(physics)" title="Quintessence (physics)">Quintessence (physics)</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Quintessence_(physics)" title="Quintessence (physics)">quintessence</a> models of dark energy, the observed acceleration of the scale factor is caused by the potential energy of a dynamical <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field" title="Scalar field">field</a>, referred to as quintessence field. Quintessence differs from the cosmological constant in that it can vary in space and time. In order for it not to clump and form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">structure</a> like matter, the field must be very light so that it has a large <a href="/wiki/Compton_wavelength" title="Compton wavelength">Compton wavelength</a>.\n</p><p>No evidence of quintessence is yet available, but it has not been ruled out either. It generally predicts a slightly slower acceleration of the expansion of the universe than the cosmological constant. Some scientists think that the best evidence for quintessence would come from violations of Einstein\'s <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle#Tests_of_the_Einstein_equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">variation of the fundamental constants</a> in space or time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll1998_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll1998-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field" title="Scalar field">Scalar fields</a> are predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle physics and <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>, but an analogous problem to the cosmological constant problem (or the problem of constructing models of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological_inflation" title="Cosmological inflation">cosmological inflation</a>) occurs: <a href="/wiki/Renormalization" title="Renormalization">renormalization</a> theory predicts that scalar fields should acquire large masses.\n</p><p>The coincidence problem asks why the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">acceleration</a> of the Universe began when it did.  If acceleration began earlier in the universe, structures such as <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> would never have had time to form, and life, at least as we know it, would never have had a chance to exist. Proponents of the <a href="/wiki/Anthropic_principle" title="Anthropic principle">anthropic principle</a> view this as support for their arguments. However, many models of quintessence have a so-called "tracker" behavior, which solves this problem. In these models, the quintessence field has a density which closely tracks (but is less than) the radiation density until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bang" title="Big bang">matter-radiation equality</a>, which triggers quintessence to start behaving as dark energy, eventually dominating the universe. This naturally sets the low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_scale" title="Energy scale">energy scale</a> of the dark energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2004, when scientists fit the evolution of dark energy with the cosmological data, they found that the equation of state had possibly crossed the cosmological constant boundary (w&#160;=&#160;&#8722;1) from above to below. A No-Go theorem has been proved that gives this scenario at least two degrees of freedom as required for dark energy models. This scenario is so-called <a href="/wiki/Quintom_scenario" title="Quintom scenario">Quintom scenario</a>.\n</p><p>Some special cases of quintessence are <a href="/wiki/Phantom_energy" title="Phantom energy">phantom energy</a>, in which the energy density of quintessence actually increases with time, and k-essence (short for kinetic quintessence) which has a non-standard form of <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> such as a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Negative_kinetic_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Negative kinetic energy (page does not exist)">negative kinetic energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> They can have unusual properties: <a href="/wiki/Phantom_energy" title="Phantom energy">phantom energy</a>, for example, can cause a <a href="/wiki/Big_Rip" title="Big Rip">Big Rip</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interacting_dark_energy">Interacting dark energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Interacting dark energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>This class of theories attempts to come up with an all-encompassing theory of both dark matter and dark energy as a single phenomenon that modifies the laws of gravity at various scales. This could, for example, treat dark energy and dark matter as different facets of the same unknown substance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> or postulate that cold dark matter decays into dark energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Another class of theories that unifies dark matter and dark energy are suggested to be covariant theories of modified gravities. These theories alter the dynamics of the space-time such that the modified dynamic stems what have been assigned to the presence of dark energy and dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variable_dark_energy_models">Variable dark energy models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Variable dark energy models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The density of dark energy might have varied in time over the history of the universe. Modern observational data allow for estimates of the present density. Using <a href="/wiki/Baryon_acoustic_oscillations" title="Baryon acoustic oscillations">baryon acoustic oscillations</a>, it is possible to investigate the effect of dark energy in the history of the Universe, and constrain parameters of the <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state" title="Equation of state">equation of state</a> of dark energy. To that end, several models have been proposed. One of the most popular models is the Chevallier&#8211;Polarski&#8211;Linder model (CPL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Some other common models are, (Barboza &amp; Alcaniz. 2008),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> (Jassal et al. 2005),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> (Wetterich. 2004),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>(Oztas et al. 2018).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_skepticism">Observational skepticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Observational skepticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some alternatives to dark energy aim to explain the observational data by a more refined use of established theories. In this scenario, dark energy doesn\'t actually exist, and is merely a measurement artifact. For example, if we are located in an emptier-than-average region of space, the observed cosmic expansion rate could be mistaken for a variation in time, or acceleration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> A different approach uses a cosmological extension of the <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a> to show how space might appear to be expanding more rapidly in the voids surrounding our local cluster. While weak, such effects considered cumulatively over billions of years could become significant, creating the illusion of cosmic acceleration, and making it appear as if we live in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Bubble_(astronomy)" title="Hubble Bubble (astronomy)">Hubble bubble</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Yet other possibilities are that the accelerated expansion of the universe is an illusion caused by the relative motion of us to the rest of the universe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> or that the supernovae sample size used wasn\'t large enough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarkar_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarkar-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ox.ac.uk_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ox.ac.uk-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_mechanism_driving_acceleration">Other mechanism driving acceleration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other mechanism driving acceleration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modified_gravity">Modified gravity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modified gravity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The evidence for dark energy is heavily dependent on the theory of general relativity. Therefore, it is conceivable that a modification to general relativity also eliminates the need for dark energy. There are very many such theories, and research is ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The measurement of the speed of gravity in the first gravitational wave measured by non-gravitational means (<a href="/wiki/GW170817" title="GW170817">GW170817</a>) ruled out many modified gravity theories as explanations to dark energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Astrophysicist <a href="/wiki/Ethan_Siegel" title="Ethan Siegel">Ethan Siegel</a> states that, while such alternatives gain a lot of mainstream press coverage, almost all professional astrophysicists are confident that dark energy exists, and that none of the competing theories successfully explain observations to the same level of precision as standard dark energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Implications_for_the_fate_of_the_universe">Implications for the fate of the universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Implications for the fate of the universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Cosmologists estimate that the <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">acceleration</a> began roughly 5 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frieman_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frieman-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[notes 1]</a></sup> Before that, it is thought that the expansion was decelerating, due to the attractive influence of matter. The density of dark matter in an expanding universe decreases more quickly than dark energy, and eventually the dark energy dominates. Specifically, when the volume of the universe doubles, the density of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> is halved, but the density of dark energy is nearly unchanged (it is exactly constant in the case of a cosmological constant).\n</p><p>Projections into the future can differ radically for different models of dark energy. For a cosmological constant, or any other model that predicts that the acceleration will continue indefinitely, the ultimate result will be that galaxies outside the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a> will have a <a href="/wiki/Radial_velocity" title="Radial velocity">line-of-sight velocity</a> that continually increases with time, eventually far exceeding the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> This is not a violation of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> because the notion of "velocity" used here is different from that of velocity in a local <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">inertial frame of reference</a>, which is still constrained to be less than the speed of light for any massive object (see <a href="/wiki/Comoving_and_proper_distances#Uses_of_the_proper_distance" title="Comoving and proper distances">Uses of the proper distance</a> for a discussion of the subtleties of defining any notion of relative velocity in cosmology). Because the <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law#Interpretation" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble parameter</a> is decreasing with time, there can actually be cases where a galaxy that is receding from us faster than light does manage to emit a signal which reaches us eventually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ly93_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ly93-81">[80]</a></sup> However, because of the accelerating expansion, it is projected that most galaxies will eventually cross a type of cosmological <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">event horizon</a> where any light they emit past that point will never be able to reach us at any time in the infinite future<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> because the light never reaches a point where its "peculiar velocity" toward us exceeds the expansion velocity away from us (these two notions of velocity are also discussed in <a href="/wiki/Comoving_and_proper_distances#Uses_of_the_proper_distance" title="Comoving and proper distances">Uses of the proper distance</a>). Assuming the dark energy is constant (a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>), the current distance to this cosmological event horizon is about 16 billion light years, meaning that a signal from an event happening <i>at present</i> would eventually be able to reach us in the future if the event were less than 16 billion light years away, but the signal would never reach us if the event were more than 16 billion light years away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ly93_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-ly93-81">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As galaxies approach the point of crossing this cosmological event horizon, the light from them will become more and more <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifted</a>, to the point where the wavelength becomes too large to detect in practice and the galaxies appear to vanish completely<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> (<i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Future_of_an_expanding_universe" title="Future of an expanding universe">Future of an expanding universe</a>). Planet Earth, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, and the Local Group of which the Milky way is a part, would all remain virtually undisturbed as the rest of the universe recedes and disappears from view. In this scenario, the Local Group would ultimately suffer <a href="/wiki/Heat_death_of_the_universe" title="Heat death of the universe">heat death</a>, just as was hypothesized for the flat, matter-dominated universe before measurements of <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_universe" title="Accelerating expansion of the universe">cosmic acceleration</a>.\n</p><p>There are other, more speculative ideas about the future of the universe. The <a href="/wiki/Phantom_energy" title="Phantom energy">phantom energy</a> model of dark energy results in <i>divergent</i> expansion, which would imply that the effective force of dark energy continues growing until it dominates all other forces in the universe. Under this scenario, dark energy would ultimately tear apart all gravitationally bound structures, including galaxies and solar systems, and eventually overcome the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_force" title="Electric force">electrical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear forces</a> to tear apart atoms themselves, ending the universe in a "<a href="/wiki/Big_Rip" title="Big Rip">Big Rip</a>". It is also possible the universe may never have an end and continue in its present state forever<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann#Second_thermodynamics_law_as_a_law_of_disorder" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">The second thermodynamics law as a law of disorder</a>). On the other hand, dark energy might dissipate with time or even become attractive. Such uncertainties leave open the possibility that gravity might yet rule the day and lead to a universe that contracts in on itself in a "<a href="/wiki/Big_Crunch" title="Big Crunch">Big Crunch</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-85">[84]</a></sup> or that there may even be a dark energy cycle, which implies a <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_model" title="Cyclic model">cyclic model of the universe</a> in which every iteration (<a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> then eventually a <a href="/wiki/Big_Crunch" title="Big Crunch">Big Crunch</a>) takes about a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000000_(number)" title="1000000000000 (number)">trillion</a> (10<sup>12</sup>) years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steinhardt_&amp;_Turok_2002_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steinhardt_&amp;_Turok_2002-87">[86]</a></sup> While none of these are supported by observations, they are not ruled out.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_philosophy_of_science">In philosophy of science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In philosophy of science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a>, dark energy is an example of an "auxiliary hypothesis", an <a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc_hypothesis" title="Ad hoc hypothesis">ad hoc</a> postulate that is added to a theory in response to observations that <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsify</a> it. It has been argued that the dark energy hypothesis is a <a href="/wiki/Conventionalism#Epistemology" title="Conventionalism">conventionalist</a> hypothesis, that is, a hypothesis that adds no empirical content and hence is <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">unfalsifiable</a> in the sense defined by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Conformal_gravity" title="Conformal gravity">Conformal gravity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dark_Energy_Spectroscopic_Instrument" title="Dark Energy Spectroscopic Instrument">Dark Energy Spectroscopic Instrument</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_Matter" title="Dark Matter">Dark Matter</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Sitter_relativity" title="De Sitter relativity">De Sitter relativity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Illustris_project" title="Illustris project">Illustris project</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Negative_mass" title="Negative mass">Negative mass</a></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Quintessence:_The_Search_for_Missing_Mass_in_the_Universe" title="Quintessence: The Search for Missing Mass in the Universe">Quintessence: The Search for Missing Mass in the Universe</a></i></li>\n<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Dark_Energy_Survey" title="The Dark Energy Survey">The Dark Energy Survey</a></i></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Vacuum_state" title="Vacuum state">Vacuum state</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frieman_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frieman-77">[77]</a></sup> Frieman, Turner &amp; Huterer (2008) p. 6: "The Universe has gone through three distinct eras: radiation-dominated, <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> &#8819; 3000</span>; matter-dominated, <span class="texhtml">3000 &#8819; <i>z</i> &#8819; 0.5</span>; and dark-energy-dominated, <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> &#8818; 0.5</span>. The evolution of the scale factor is controlled by the dominant energy form: <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i>(<i>t</i>) &#8733; <i>t</i><sup>2/3</sup>(1 + <i>w</i>)</span> (for constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">w</span>). During the radiation-dominated era, <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i>(<i>t</i>) &#8733; <i>t</i><sup>1/2</sup></span>; during the matter-dominated era, <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i>(<i>t</i>) &#8733; <i>t</i><sup>2/3</sup></span>; and for the dark energy-dominated era, assuming <span class="texhtml"><i>w</i> = &#8722;1</span>, asymptotically <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i>(<i>t</i>) &#8733; exp(<i>Ht</i>)</span>."<br/>\np. 44: "Taken together, all the current data provide strong evidence for the existence of dark energy; they constrain the fraction of critical density contributed by dark energy, 0.76&#160;&#177;&#160;0.02, and the equation-of-state parameter, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">w</span>&#160;&#8776; &#8722;1&#160;&#177;&#160;0.1 (stat) &#177;&#160;0.1 (sys), assuming that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">w</span> is constant. This implies that the Universe began accelerating at redshift <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> &#8764;</span>&#160;0.4 and age <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i> &#8764;</span>&#160;10&#160;Gyr. These results are robust &#8211; data from any one method can be removed without compromising the constraints &#8211; and they are not substantially weakened by dropping the assumption of spatial flatness."</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-NYT-20170220-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-NYT-20170220_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a href="/wiki/Dennis_Overbye" title="Dennis Overbye">Overbye, Dennis</a> (20 February 2017). <a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/02/20/science/hubble-constant-universe-expanding-speed.html" rel="nofollow">"Cosmos Controversy: The Universe Is Expanding, but How Fast?"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">.
